<p><b>Earth</b> is the third <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the only object in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> known to harbor <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a> and other sources of evidence, Earth <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formed</a> over 4 <a href="/wiki/Bya" title="Bya">billion years ago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS1997_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1997-28">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">Earth's gravity</a> interacts with other objects in space, especially the Sun and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, Earth's only <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellite</a>. Earth <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">revolves around the Sun</a> in 365.26 days, a period known as an Earth <a href="/wiki/Year" title="Year">year</a>. During this time, Earth <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">rotates about its axis</a> about 366.26 times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sidereal_solar_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-sidereal_solar-31">[n 5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Earth's <a href="#Axial_tilt_and_seasons">axis of rotation</a> is tilted, producing <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">seasonal variations</a> on the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoder1995_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-yoder1995-32">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational interaction</a> between the Earth and Moon causes <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">ocean tides</a>, stabilizes the Earth's orientation on its axis, and gradually slows its rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa428_261_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa428_261-33">[28]</a></sup> Earth is the densest planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and the largest of the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithosphere#Earth.27s_lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">Earth's lithosphere</a> is divided into several rigid <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic plates</a> that migrate across the surface over periods of many millions of years. About 71% of Earth's surface is <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">covered with water</a>, mostly by its <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup> The remaining 29% is <a href="/wiki/Land#History_of_land_on_Earth" title="Land">land</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> that together have many lakes, rivers and other sources of water that contribute to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>. The majority of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth" title="Polar regions of Earth">polar regions</a> are covered in ice, including the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> and the sea ice of the Arctic ice pack. Earth's interior remains active with a solid iron inner core, a liquid outer core that generates the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a>, and a convecting <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> that drives plate tectonics.</p>
<p>Within the first billion years of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">Earth's history</a>, life appeared in the oceans and began to affect the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> and surface, leading to the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic organisms</a>. Some geological evidence indicates that life may have arisen as much as 4.1 billion years ago. Since then, the combination of Earth's distance from the Sun, physical properties, and <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">geological history</a> have allowed life to evolve and thrive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-36">[31]</a></sup> In the history of the Earth, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> has gone through long periods of expansion, occasionally punctuated by <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinction events</a>. Over 99% of all <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book-Biology_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Biology-37">[32]</a></sup> that ever lived on Earth are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-38">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-39">[34]</a></sup> Estimates of the number of species on Earth today vary widely;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science_241_4872_1441_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-science_241_4872_1441-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-41">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF-2016002_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-2016002-42">[37]</a></sup> most species have not been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLoS-20110823_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-20110823-43">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">Over 7.4 billion humans</a> live on Earth and depend on its <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_resources" title="Natural resources">natural resources</a> for their survival. Humans have developed diverse <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">societies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>; politically, the world has <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states" title="List of sovereign states">about 200 sovereign states</a>.</p>
<p></p>

</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_etymology">Name and etymology</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">modern English</a> word <span id="Name"></span><span id="Etymology"></span> <i>Earth</i> developed from a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> forms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[n 6]</a></sup> which derived from an <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> noun most often spelled <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eor%C3%B0e" title="wikt:eorðe">eorðe</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup> It has cognates in every <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic language</a>, and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">proto-Germanic</a> root has been reconstructed as <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Germanic/er%C3%BE%C5%8D" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Germanic/erþō">*<i>erþō</i></a>. In its earliest appearances, <i>eorðe</i> was already being used to translate the many senses of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/terra" title="wikt:terra">terra</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%E1%BF%86" title="wikt:γῆ">γῆ</a> (<i>gē</i>): the ground,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[n 7]</a></sup> its <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[n 8]</a></sup> dry land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[n 9]</a></sup> the human world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[n 10]</a></sup> the surface of the world (including the sea),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[n 11]</a></sup> and the globe itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[n 12]</a></sup> As with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terra_(goddess)" title="Terra (goddess)">Terra</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(goddess)" title="Gaia (goddess)">Gaia</a>, Earth was a <a href="/wiki/Earth_goddess" title="Earth goddess">personified goddess</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> were listed by <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> as among the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_paganism" title="Anglo-Saxon paganism">devotees</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nerthus" title="Nerthus">Nerthus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[48]</a></sup> and later <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a> included <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6r%C3%B0" title="Jörð">Jörð</a>, a giantess often given as the mother of <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIMEK179_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIMEK179-61">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Originally, <i>earth</i> was written in lowercase, and from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Middle_English" title="Early Middle English">early Middle English</a>, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite" title="Definite">definite</a> sense as "the globe" was expressed as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">the</a> earth</i>. By <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">early Modern English</a>, many nouns were capitalized, and <i>the earth</i> became (and often remained) <i>the Earth</i>, particularly when referenced along with other heavenly bodies. More recently, the name is sometimes simply given as <i>Earth</i>, by analogy with the names of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">other planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-8"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Style_guide" title="Style guide">House styles</a> now vary: <a href="/wiki/Oxford_spelling" title="Oxford spelling">Oxford spelling</a> recognizes the lowercase form as the most common, with the capitalized form an acceptable variant. Another convention capitalizes "Earth" when appearing as a name (e.g. "Earth's atmosphere") but writes it in lowercase when preceded by <i>the</i> (e.g. "the atmosphere of the earth"). It almost always appears in lowercase in colloquial expressions such as "what on earth are you doing?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-62">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">History of Earth</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Protoplanetary-disk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="312" data-file-width="576" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Protoplanetary-disk.jpg/220px-Protoplanetary-disk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Protoplanetary-disk.jpg/330px-Protoplanetary-disk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Protoplanetary-disk.jpg/440px-Protoplanetary-disk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Protoplanetary-disk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's impression of the early Solar System's planetary disk</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oldest material found in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is dated to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000456720000000000♠</span>4.5672<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.0006 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billion_years_ago" title="Billion years ago">billion years ago</a></span> (Bya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowring_housch1995_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowring_housch1995-63">[51]</a></sup> By <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000454000000000000♠</span>4.54<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.04 Bya</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_earth1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-age_earth1-64">[52]</a></sup> the primordial Earth had formed. The <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation and evolution of Solar System</a> bodies occurred along with the Sun. In theory, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> partitions a volume out of a <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a> by gravitational collapse, which begins to spin and flatten into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disk" title="Circumstellar disk">circumstellar disk</a>, and then the planets grow out of that disk along with the Sun. A nebula contains gas, ice grains, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a>). According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nebular_theory" title="Nebular theory">nebular theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>, with the primordial Earth taking 10–<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001200000000000000♠</span>20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Million_years" title="Million years">million years</a></span> (Mys) to form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature418_6901_949_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature418_6901_949-65">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>A subject of on-going research is the formation of the Moon, some 4.53 Bya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science310_5754_1671_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-science310_5754_1671-66">[54]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Working_hypothesis" title="Working hypothesis">working hypothesis</a> is that it was formed by accretion from material loosed from Earth after a <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>-sized object, named <a href="/wiki/Theia_(planet)" title="Theia (planet)">Theia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">impacted</a> Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reilly20091022_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-reilly20091022-67">[55]</a></sup> In this scenario, the mass of Theia was approximately 10% of that of Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canup_asphaug2001a_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-canup_asphaug2001a-68">[56]</a></sup> it impacted Earth with a glancing blow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canup_asphaug2001b_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-canup_asphaug2001b-69">[57]</a></sup> and some of its mass merged with Earth. Between approximately 4.1 and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000380000000000000♠</span>3.8 Bya</span>, numerous <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> impacts during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> caused significant changes to the greater surface environment of the Moon, and by inference, to that of Earth.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_history">Geological history</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">Geological history of Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/220px-USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/330px-USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/440px-USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USA_10654_Bryce_Canyon_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hoodoo_(geology)" title="Hoodoo (geology)">Hoodoos</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Bryce_Canyon_National_Park" title="Bryce Canyon National Park">Bryce Canyon National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth's atmosphere and oceans were formed by <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic activity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> that included water vapor. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_world%27s_oceans" title="Origin of the world's oceans">origin of the world's oceans</a> was condensation augmented by water and ice delivered by asteroids, <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watersource_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-watersource-70">[58]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox" title="Faint young Sun paradox">this model</a>, atmospheric "greenhouse gases" kept the oceans from freezing when the newly forming Sun had only 70% of its <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">current luminosity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asp2002_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-asp2002-71">[59]</a></sup> By <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000350000000000000♠</span>3.5 Bya</span>, <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> was established, which helped prevent the atmosphere from being stripped away by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physorg20100304_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-physorg20100304-72">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>A crust formed when the molten outer layer of Earth cooled <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">to form</a> a solid. The two models<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams_santosh2004_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams_santosh2004-73">[61]</a></sup> that explain land mass propose either a steady growth to the present-day forms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science164_1229_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-science164_1229-74">[62]</a></sup> or, more likely, a rapid growth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tp322_19_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-tp322_19-75">[63]</a></sup> early in Earth history<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg6_175_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-rg6_175-76">[64]</a></sup> followed by a long-term steady continental area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science310_5756_1947_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-science310_5756_1947-77">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaes23_799_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-jaes23_799-78">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajes38_613_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-ajes38_613-79">[67]</a></sup> Continents formed by <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, a process ultimately driven by the continuous loss of heat from Earth's interior. On <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">time scales</a> lasting hundreds of millions of years, the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinents</a> have assembled and broken apart. Roughly <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002750000000000000♠</span>750 million years ago</span> (Mya), one of the earliest known supercontinents, <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a>, began to break apart. The continents later recombined to form <a href="/wiki/Pannotia" title="Pannotia">Pannotia</a>, 600–<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002540000000000000♠</span>540 Mya</span>, then finally <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, which also broke apart <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002180000000000000♠</span>180 Mya</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-as92_324_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-as92_324-80">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The present pattern of <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a> began about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001400000000000000♠</span>40 Mya</span> and then intensified during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000300000000000000♠</span>3 Mya</span>. High-<a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> regions have since undergone repeated cycles of glaciation and thaw, repeating about every 40,000–<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7012315576000000000♠</span>100<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span> years</span>. The last continental glaciation ended 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psc_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-psc-81">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_life_and_evolution">Origin of life and evolution</span></h3>
<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years</a>.<br/>
<small><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><span style="color:black;">Orange labels:</span> <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation"><span style="color:black;">known <i><b>ice ages</b></i>.</span></a></span></small><br/>
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history of life</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PhylogeneticTree,_Woese_1990.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1257" data-file-width="1842" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG/220px-PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG/330px-PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG/440px-PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PhylogeneticTree,_Woese_1990.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Speculative <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> of life on Earth based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RRNA" title="RRNA">rRNA</a> analysis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">Chemical reactions</a> led to the first self-replicating molecules about four billion years ago. A half billion years later, the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last common ancestor of all life</a> arose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa282_6_90_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa282_6_90-82">[70]</a></sup> The evolution of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> allowed the Sun's energy to be harvested directly by life forms. The resultant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" title="Molecular oxygen">molecular oxygen</a> (O<sub>2</sub>) accumulated in the atmosphere and due to interaction with ultraviolet solar radiation, formed a protective <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> (O<sub>3</sub>) in the upper atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-83">[71]</a></sup> The incorporation of smaller cells within larger ones resulted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" title="Endosymbiotic theory">development of complex cells</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotes" title="Eukaryotes">eukaryotes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas22_3_225_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-jas22_3_225-84">[72]</a></sup> True multicellular organisms formed as cells within <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> became increasingly specialized. Aided by the absorption of harmful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> by the ozone layer, life colonized Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burton20021129_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-burton20021129-85">[73]</a></sup> Among the earliest <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> evidence for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> is <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> fossils found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin1-86">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin2_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-87">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-88">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-89">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-90">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> found in 3.7 billion-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">metasedimentary rocks</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-91">[79]</a></sup> remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-36">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neoproterozoic" title="Neoproterozoic">Neoproterozoic</a>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002750000000000000♠</span>750 to 580 Mya</span>, much of Earth might have been covered in ice. This hypothesis has been termed "<a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a>", and it is of particular interest because it preceded the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, when multicellular life forms significantly increased in complexity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirschvink1992_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-kirschvink1992-92">[80]</a></sup> Following the Cambrian explosion, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002535000000000000♠</span>535 Mya</span>, there have been five <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">major mass extinctions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci215_4539_1501_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci215_4539_1501-93">[81]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Tertiary_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Tertiary extinction event">most recent such event</a> was <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001660000000000000♠</span>66 Mya</span>, when <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_impactor" title="Chicxulub impactor">an asteroid impact</a> triggered the extinction of the non-<a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">avian</a> <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and other large reptiles, but spared some small animals such as <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, which then resembled <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a>. Over the past <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001660000000000000♠</span>66 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mys&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mys (page does not exist)">Mys</a></span>, mammalian life has diversified, and several million years ago an African ape-like animal such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orrorin_tugenensis" title="Orrorin tugenensis">Orrorin tugenensis</a></i> gained the ability to stand upright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gould1994_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-gould1994-94">[82]</a></sup> This facilitated tool use and encouraged communication that provided the nutrition and stimulation needed for a larger brain, which allowed the <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">evolution of humans</a>. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">development of agriculture</a>, and then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_civilizations" title="List of ancient civilizations">civilization</a>, led to humans having an <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">influence on Earth</a> and the nature and quantity of other life forms that continues today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bgsa119_1_140_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-bgsa119_1_140-95">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Earth" title="Future of Earth">Future of Earth</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk" title="Global catastrophic risk">Global catastrophic risk</a></div>
<p>Earth's expected long-term future is closely tied to that of the Sun. Over the next <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000110000000000000♠</span>1.1 Bys</span>, solar luminosity will increase by 10%, and over the next <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000350000000000000♠</span>3.5 Bys</span> by 40%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-96">[84]</a></sup> The Earth's increasing surface temperature will accelerate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inorganic" title="Inorganic">inorganic</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">CO<sub>2</sub> cycle</a>, reducing its concentration to levels lethally low for plants (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6995099999999999999♠</span>10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a></span> for <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 photosynthesis</a>) in approximately 500–<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002900000000000000♠</span>900 Mys</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britt2000_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-britt2000-97">[85]</a></sup> The lack of vegetation will result in the loss of oxygen in the atmosphere, and animal life will become extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ward_brownlee2002_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ward_brownlee2002-98">[86]</a></sup> After another billion years all surface water will have disappeared<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrington_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-carrington-99">[87]</a></sup> and the mean global temperature will reach <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002343150000000000♠</span>70 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ward_brownlee2002_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-ward_brownlee2002-98">[86]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002343150000000000♠</span>158 <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a></span>). From that point, the Earth is expected to be habitable for another <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7016157788000000000♠</span>500 Ma</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britt2000_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-britt2000-97">[85]</a></sup> possibly up to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7016725824800000000♠</span>2.3 Ga</span> if nitrogen is removed from the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_24_9576_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1_24_9576-100">[88]</a></sup> Even if the Sun were eternal and stable, 27% of the water in the modern oceans will descend to the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> in one billion years, due to reduced steam venting from mid-ocean ridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hess5_4_569_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-hess5_4_569-101">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun will <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolve</a> to become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> in about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000500000000000000♠</span>5 Bys</span>. Models predict that the Sun will expand to roughly 1 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (150 million km; 93 million mi), which is about 250 times its present radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-96">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_schroder_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future_schroder-102">[90]</a></sup> Earth's fate is less clear. As a red giant, the Sun will lose roughly 30% of its mass, so, without tidal effects, Earth will move to an orbit 1.7 AU (250 million km; 160 million mi) from the Sun when the star reaches its maximum radius. Most, if not all, remaining life will be destroyed by the Sun's increased luminosity (peaking at about 5,000 times its present level).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-96">[84]</a></sup> A 2008 simulation indicates that Earth's orbit will eventually decay due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal effects</a> and drag, causing it to enter the Sun's atmosphere and be vaporized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_schroder_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future_schroder-102">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shape">Shape</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1362" data-file-width="1500" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/220px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/330px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/440px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shape of planet Earth. Shown are distances between surface relief and the geocentre. The South American Andes summits are visible as elevated areas. Data from the Earth2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth2014_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Earth2014-103">[91]</a></sup> global relief model.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth" title="Figure of the Earth">Figure of the Earth</a></div>
<p>The shape of Earth is approximately <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroidal</a>. Due to <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotation</a>, the Earth is flattened along the geographic axis and <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bulge" title="Equatorial bulge">bulging</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milbert_smith96_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-milbert_smith96-104">[92]</a></sup> The diameter of the Earth at the equator is 43 kilometres (27 mi) larger than the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">pole</a>-to-pole diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngdc2006_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ngdc2006-105">[93]</a></sup> Thus the point on the surface farthest from Earth's <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> is the summit of the equatorial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chimborazo_(volcano)" title="Chimborazo (volcano)">Chimborazo</a> volcano in <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps20_5_16_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-ps20_5_16-106">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lancet365_9462_831_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-lancet365_9462_831-107">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tall_tales_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-tall_tales-108">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_.27Highest.27_Spot_on_Earth_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_.27Highest.27_Spot_on_Earth-109">[97]</a></sup> The average diameter of the reference spheroid is 12,742 kilometres (7,918 mi). Local <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> deviates from this idealized spheroid, although on a global scale these deviations are small compared to Earth's radius: The maximum deviation of only 0.17% is at the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a> (10,911 metres (35,797 ft) below local sea level), whereas <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> (8,848 metres (29,029 ft) above local sea level) represents a deviation of 0.14%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[n 13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>, the exact shape that Earth's oceans would adopt in the absence of land and perturbations such as tides and winds is called the <a href="/wiki/Geoid" title="Geoid">geoid</a>. More precisely, the geoid is the surface of gravitational equipotential at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_sea_level" title="Mean sea level">mean sea level</a>.</p>

</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a></td>

<td style="text-align: right;">1.2%</td>

<td style="text-align: center;">TiO<sub>2</sub></td>

<th style="text-align: right;">99.9%</th>
</tr>

<p><a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass">Earth's mass</a> is approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7024597000000000000♠</span>5.97<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>24</sup> <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a></span> (5,970 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yottagram" title="Yottagram">Yg</a>). It is composed mostly of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (32.1%), <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (30.1%), <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (15.1%), <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> (13.9%), <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> (2.9%), <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> (1.8%), <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> (1.5%), and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> (1.4%), with the remaining 1.2% consisting of trace amounts of other elements. Due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_segregation" title="Mass segregation">mass segregation</a>, the core region is estimated to be primarily composed of iron (88.8%), with smaller amounts of nickel (5.8%), sulfur (4.5%), and less than 1% trace elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas71_12_6973_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas71_12_6973-113">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most common rock constituents of the crust are nearly all oxides: chlorine, sulfur, and fluorine are the important exceptions to this and their total amount in any rock is usually much less than 1%. Over 99% of the crust is composed of 11 oxides, principally silica, alumina, iron oxides, lime, magnesia, potash, and soda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_mussett1981_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-brown_mussett1981-112">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas71_12_6973_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-pnas71_12_6973-113">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">Structure of the Earth</a></div>
<p>Earth's interior, like that of the other terrestrial planets, is divided into layers by their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> or physical (<a href="/wiki/Rheology" title="Rheology">rheological</a>) properties. The outer layer is a chemically distinct <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate</a> solid crust, which is underlain by a highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscous" title="Viscous">viscous</a> solid mantle. The crust is separated from the mantle by the <a href="/wiki/Mohorovi%C4%8Di%C4%87_discontinuity" title="Mohorovičić discontinuity">Mohorovičić discontinuity</a>. The thickness of the crust varies from about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003600000000000000♠</span>6 <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a></span> (kilometers) under the oceans to 30–50 km for the continents. The crust and the cold, rigid, top of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_mantle" title="Upper mantle">upper mantle</a> are collectively known as the lithosphere, and it is of the lithosphere that the tectonic plates are composed. Beneath the lithosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Asthenosphere" title="Asthenosphere">asthenosphere</a>, a relatively low-viscosity layer on which the lithosphere rides. Important changes in crystal structure within the mantle occur at 410 and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005660000000000000♠</span>660 km</span> below the surface, spanning a <a href="/wiki/Transition_zone_(Earth)" title="Transition zone (Earth)">transition zone</a> that separates the upper and lower mantle. Beneath the mantle, an extremely low viscosity liquid <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer core</a> lies above a solid <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner core</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tanimoto_ahrens1995_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-tanimoto_ahrens1995-115">[102]</a></sup> The Earth's inner core might rotate at a slightly higher <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a> than the remainder of the planet, advancing by 0.1–0.5° per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science309_5739_1313_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-science309_5739_1313-116">[103]</a></sup> The radius of the inner core is about one fifth of that of Earth.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat">Heat</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_internal_heat_budget" title="Earth's internal heat budget">Earth's internal heat budget</a></div>
<p>Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_heat" title="Internal heat">internal heat</a> comes from a combination of residual heat from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_accretion" title="Planetary accretion">planetary accretion</a> (about 20%) and heat produced through <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> (80%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turcotte_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-turcotte-121">[106]</a></sup> The major heat-producing <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> within Earth are <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">potassium-40</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232">thorium-232</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanders20031210_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanders20031210-122">[107]</a></sup> At the center, the temperature may be up to 6,000 °C (10,830 °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[108]</a></sup> and the pressure could reach 360 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPa" title="GPa">GPa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ptrsl360_1795_1227_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-ptrsl360_1795_1227-124">[109]</a></sup> Because much of the heat is provided by radioactive decay, scientists postulate that early in Earth's history, before isotopes with short half-lives were depleted, Earth's heat production was much higher. At approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7016946728000000000♠</span>3 <a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">Ga</a></span>, twice the present-day heat would have been produced, increasing the rates of <a href="/wiki/Mantle_convection" title="Mantle convection">mantle convection</a> and plate tectonics, and allowing the production of uncommon igneous rocks such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komatiites" title="Komatiites">komatiites</a> that are rarely formed today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turcotte_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-turcotte-121">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epsl121_1_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-epsl121_1-125">[110]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">

<p>The mean heat loss from Earth is <span class="nowrap">87 mW m<sup>−2</sup></span>, for a global heat loss of <span class="nowrap">4.42 × 10<sup>13</sup> W</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jg31_3_267_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-jg31_3_267-127">[112]</a></sup> A portion of the core's thermal energy is transported toward the crust by <a href="/wiki/Mantle_plume" title="Mantle plume">mantle plumes</a>, a form of convection consisting of upwellings of higher-temperature rock. These plumes can produce <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">flood basalts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science246_4926_103_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-science246_4926_103-128">[113]</a></sup> More of the heat in Earth is lost through plate tectonics, by mantle upwelling associated with <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a>. The final major mode of heat loss is through conduction through the lithosphere, the majority of which occurs under the oceans because the crust there is much thinner than that of the continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heat_loss_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-heat_loss-129">[114]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:right;"></div>
