<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">Plate tectonics</a></div>
<p>The mechanically rigid outer layer of Earth, the lithosphere, is divided into pieces called tectonic plates. These plates are rigid segments that move in relation to one another at one of three types of plate boundaries: <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent boundaries</a>, at which two plates come together, <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundaries</a>, at which two plates are pulled apart, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transform_boundary" title="Transform boundary">transform boundaries</a>, in which two plates slide past one another laterally. <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">Earthquakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanic activity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">mountain-building</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">oceanic trench</a> formation can occur along these plate boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kious_tilling1999_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-kious_tilling1999-132">[116]</a></sup> The tectonic plates ride on top of the asthenosphere, the solid but less-viscous part of the upper mantle that can flow and move along with the plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seligman2008_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-seligman2008-133">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the tectonic plates migrate, oceanic crust is <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subducted</a> under the leading edges of the plates at convergent boundaries. At the same time, the upwelling of mantle material at divergent boundaries creates mid-ocean ridges. The combination of these processes recycles the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> back into the mantle. Due to this recycling, most of the ocean floor is less than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7015315576000000000♠</span>100 Ma</span> old in age. The oldest oceanic crust is located in the Western Pacific and has an estimated age of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7015631152000000000♠</span>200 Ma</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duennebier1999_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-duennebier1999-134">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa20070307_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaa20070307-135">[119]</a></sup> By comparison, the oldest dated <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7017127177128000000♠</span>4030 Ma</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cmp134_3_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-cmp134_3-136">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The seven major plates are the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a>. Other notable plates include the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Plate" title="Arabian Plate">Arabian Plate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Plate" title="Caribbean Plate">Caribbean Plate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Plate" title="Nazca Plate">Nazca Plate</a> off the west coast of South America and the <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Plate" title="Scotia Plate">Scotia Plate</a> in the southern Atlantic Ocean. The Australian Plate fused with the Indian Plate between 50 and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001550000000000000♠</span>55 Mya</span>. The fastest-moving plates are the oceanic plates, with the <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Plate" title="Cocos Plate">Cocos Plate</a> advancing at a rate of 75 mm/year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-podp2000_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-podp2000-137">[121]</a></sup> and the Pacific Plate moving 52–69 mm/year. At the other extreme, the slowest-moving plate is the Eurasian Plate, progressing at a typical rate of 21 mm/year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gps_time_series_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-gps_time_series-138">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface">Surface</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">Lithosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">Landform</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extreme_points_of_Earth" title="Extreme points of Earth">Extreme points of Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AYool_topography_15min.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1133" data-file-width="1617" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/AYool_topography_15min.png/220px-AYool_topography_15min.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/AYool_topography_15min.png/330px-AYool_topography_15min.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/AYool_topography_15min.png/440px-AYool_topography_15min.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AYool_topography_15min.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Present-day Earth <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">altimetry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a>. Data from the <a href="/wiki/National_Geophysical_Data_Center" title="National Geophysical Data Center">National Geophysical Data Center</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The total <a href="/wiki/Spheroid#Area" title="Spheroid">surface area</a> of the Earth is about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008510000000000000♠</span>510 million <a href="/wiki/Square_kilometre" title="Square kilometre">km<sup>2</sup></a></span> (197 million sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_2006_8_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny_2006_8-16">[13]</a></sup> Of this, 70.8%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_2006_8_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny_2006_8-16">[13]</a></sup> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008361130000000000♠</span>361.13 million km<sup>2</sup></span> (139.43 million sq mi), is below sea level and covered by ocean water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[123]</a></sup> Below the ocean's surface are much of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>, mountains, volcanoes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngdc2006_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-ngdc2006-105">[93]</a></sup> oceanic trenches, <a href="/wiki/Submarine_canyon" title="Submarine canyon">submarine canyons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_plateau" title="Oceanic plateau">oceanic plateaus</a>, abyssal plains, and a globe-spanning mid-ocean ridge system. The remaining 29.2% (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008148940000000000♠</span>148.94 million km<sup>2</sup></span>, or 57.51 million sq mi) not covered by water has <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> that varies greatly from place to place and consists of mountains, deserts, plains, plateaus, and other <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landforms</a>. <a href="/wiki/Erosion_and_tectonics" title="Erosion and tectonics">Tectonics and erosion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Types_of_volcanic_eruptions" title="Types of volcanic eruptions">volcanic eruptions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flooding" title="Flooding">flooding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">glaciation</a>, the growth of <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">meteorite impacts</a> are among the processes that constantly reshape the Earth's surface over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_time" title="Geological time">geological time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kring_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-kring-140">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>The continental crust consists of lower density material such as the igneous rocks <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andesite" title="Andesite">andesite</a>. Less common is <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>, a denser volcanic rock that is the primary constituent of the ocean floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-layers_earth_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-layers_earth-142">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">Sedimentary rock</a> is formed from the accumulation of sediment that becomes buried and <a href="/wiki/Diagenesis" title="Diagenesis">compacted together</a>. Nearly 75% of the continental surfaces are covered by sedimentary rocks, although they form about 5% of the crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jessey_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-jessey-143">[127]</a></sup> The third form of rock material found on Earth is <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a>, which is created from the transformation of pre-existing rock types through high pressures, high temperatures, or both. The most abundant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicate_mineral" title="Silicate mineral">silicate minerals</a> on Earth's surface include <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibole" title="Amphibole">amphibole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_pater_lissauer2010_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_pater_lissauer2010-144">[128]</a></sup> Common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonate_mineral" title="Carbonate mineral">carbonate minerals</a> include <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> (found in <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wekn_bulakh2004_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-wekn_bulakh2004-145">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The elevation of the land surface varies from the low point of −418 m at the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>, to a maximum altitude of 8,848 m at the top of Mount Everest. The mean height of land above sea level is about 797 metres (2,615 ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[130]</a></sup>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pedosphere" title="Pedosphere">pedosphere</a> is the outermost layer of Earth's continental surface and is composed of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> and subject to <a href="/wiki/Pedogenesis" title="Pedogenesis">soil formation processes</a>. The total arable land is 10.9% of the land surface, with 1.3% being permanent cropland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[132]</a></sup> Close to 40% of Earth's land surface is used for cropland and pasture, or an estimated 1.3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">7</span></sup> km<sup>2</sup> of cropland and 3.4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">7</span></sup> km<sup>2</sup> of pastureland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao1994_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao1994-149">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrosphere">Hydrosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">Hydrosphere</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="582" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg/220px-Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg/330px-Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg/440px-Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_elevation_histogram_2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elevation histogram of Earth's surface</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The abundance of water on Earth's surface is a unique feature that distinguishes the "Blue Planet" from other planets in the Solar System. Earth's hydrosphere consists chiefly of the oceans, but technically includes all water surfaces in the world, including inland seas, lakes, rivers, and underground waters down to a depth of 2,000 m. The deepest underwater location is <a href="/wiki/Challenger_Deep" title="Challenger Deep">Challenger Deep</a> of the Mariana Trench in the Pacific Ocean with a depth of 10,911.4 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trench_depth_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-trench_depth-150">[n 17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaiko7000_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaiko7000-151">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The mass of the oceans is approximately 1.35<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">18</span></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> or about 1/4400 of Earth's total mass. The oceans cover an area of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7014361800000000000♠</span>3.618<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>8</sup> km<sup>2</sup></span> with a mean depth of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003368200000000000♠</span>3682 m</span>, resulting in an estimated volume of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7018133200000000000♠</span>1.332<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> km<sup>3</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ocean23_2_112_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-ocean23_2_112-152">[135]</a></sup> If all of Earth's crustal surface were at the same elevation as a smooth sphere, the depth of the resulting world ocean would be 2.7 to 2.8 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 97.5% of the water is <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">saline</a>; the remaining 2.5% is <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>. Most fresh water, about 68.7%, is present as ice in <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>The average <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of Earth's oceans is about 35 grams of salt per kilogram of sea water (3.5% salt).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennish2001_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennish2001-156">[139]</a></sup> Most of this salt was released from volcanic activity or extracted from cool igneous rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mullen2002_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-mullen2002-157">[140]</a></sup> The oceans are also a reservoir of dissolved atmospheric gases, which are essential for the survival of many aquatic life forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natsci_oxy4_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-natsci_oxy4-158">[141]</a></sup> Sea water has an important influence on the world's climate, with the oceans acting as a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_reservoir" title="Heat reservoir">heat reservoir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michon2006_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-michon2006-159">[142]</a></sup> Shifts in the oceanic temperature distribution can cause significant weather shifts, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">El Niño-Southern Oscillation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sample2005_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-sample2005-160">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Atmosphere of Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MODIS_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="5400" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/MODIS_Map.jpg/220px-MODIS_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/MODIS_Map.jpg/330px-MODIS_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/MODIS_Map.jpg/440px-MODIS_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MODIS_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite image of Earth <a href="/wiki/Cloud_cover" title="Cloud cover">cloud cover</a> using <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderate-Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" title="Moderate-Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">Moderate-Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> on Earth's surface averages 101.325 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KPa" title="KPa">kPa</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Scale_height" title="Scale height">scale height</a> of about 8.5 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earth_fact_sheet_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5">[3]</a></sup> It has a composition of 78% <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and 21% oxygen, with trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, and other gaseous molecules. The height of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> varies with latitude, ranging between 8 km at the poles to 17 km at the equator, with some variation resulting from weather and seasonal factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geerts_linacre97_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-geerts_linacre97-161">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Earth's <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> has significantly altered its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a>. <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolution#Oxygen_evolution_in_nature" title="Oxygen evolution">Oxygenic photosynthesis</a> evolved <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000270000000000000♠</span>2.7 Gya</span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_catastrophe" title="Oxygen catastrophe">forming</a> the primarily nitrogen–oxygen atmosphere of today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-83">[71]</a></sup> This change enabled the proliferation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_organisms" title="Aerobic organisms">aerobic organisms</a> and, indirectly, the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> due to the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Ozone%E2%80%93oxygen_cycle" title="Ozone–oxygen cycle">conversion of atmospheric O<sub>2</sub> into O<sub>3</sub></a>. The ozone layer blocks <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>, permitting life on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_2002_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_2002-162">[145]</a></sup> Other atmospheric functions important to life include transporting water vapor, providing useful gases, causing small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor" title="Meteor">meteors</a> to burn up before they strike the surface, and moderating temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atmosphere_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-atmosphere-163">[146]</a></sup> This last phenomenon is known as the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>: trace molecules within the atmosphere serve to capture <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> emitted from the ground, thereby raising the average temperature. Water vapor, carbon dioxide, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> are the primary <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> in the atmosphere. Without this heat-retention effect, the average surface temperature would be −18 °C, in contrast to the current +15 °C,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny2006_7_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny2006_7-164">[147]</a></sup> and life on Earth probably would not exist in its current form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Narottam2008_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Narottam2008-165">[148]</a></sup> In May 2017, glints of light, seen as twinkling from an orbiting satellite a million miles away, were found to be <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflected light</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ice_crystals" title="Ice crystals">ice crystals</a> in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170519_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170519-166">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRL-201760515_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRL-201760515-167">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Weather_and_climate">Weather and climate</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">Weather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">Climate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1890" data-file-width="2772" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg/220px-Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg/330px-Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg/440px-Felix_from_ISS_03_sept_2007_1138Z.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Felix" title="Hurricane Felix">Hurricane Felix</a> seen from low Earth orbit, September 2007</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg/220px-Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg/330px-Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg/440px-Pressure_ridges_Scott_Base_lrg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lenticular_cloud" title="Lenticular cloud">Lenticular cloud</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Pressure_ridge_(ice)" title="Pressure ridge (ice)">pressure ridge</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mount_Discovery" title="Mount Discovery">Mount Discovery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, November 2013</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D-Clouds.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/3D-Clouds.jpg/220px-3D-Clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/3D-Clouds.jpg/330px-3D-Clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/3D-Clouds.jpg/440px-3D-Clouds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Massive clouds above the <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>, February 2016</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth's atmosphere has no definite boundary, slowly becoming thinner and fading into outer space. Three-quarters of the atmosphere's mass is contained within the first 11 km (6.8 mi) of the surface. This lowest layer is called the troposphere. Energy from the Sun heats this layer, and the surface below, causing expansion of the air. This lower-density air then rises and is replaced by cooler, higher-density air. The result is <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">atmospheric circulation</a> that drives the weather and climate through redistribution of thermal energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moran2005_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-moran2005-168">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>The primary atmospheric circulation bands consist of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a> in the equatorial region below 30° latitude and the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">westerlies</a> in the mid-latitudes between 30° and 60°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berger2002_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-berger2002-169">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">Ocean currents</a> are also important factors in determining climate, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> that distributes thermal energy from the equatorial oceans to the polar regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rahmstorf2003_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-rahmstorf2003-170">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water vapor generated through surface evaporation is transported by circulatory patterns in the atmosphere. When atmospheric conditions permit an uplift of warm, humid air, this water condenses and falls to the surface as precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moran2005_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-moran2005-168">[151]</a></sup> Most of the water is then transported to lower elevations by river systems and usually returned to the oceans or deposited into lakes. This <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> is a vital mechanism for supporting life on land and is a primary factor in the erosion of surface features over geological periods. Precipitation patterns vary widely, ranging from several meters of water per year to less than a millimeter. Atmospheric circulation, topographic features, and temperature differences determine the average precipitation that falls in each region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydrologic_cycle_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-hydrologic_cycle-171">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The amount of solar energy reaching Earth's surface decreases with increasing latitude. At higher latitudes, the sunlight reaches the surface at lower angles, and it must pass through thicker columns of the atmosphere. As a result, the mean annual air temperature at sea level decreases by about 0.4 °C (0.7 °F) per degree of latitude from the equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadava_heller2006_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-sadava_heller2006-172">[155]</a></sup> Earth's surface can be subdivided into specific latitudinal belts of approximately homogeneous climate. Ranging from the equator to the polar regions, these are the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> (or equatorial), <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar</a> climates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-climate_zones_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-climate_zones-173">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>This latitudinal rule has several anomalies:</p>
<ul>
<li>Proximity to oceans moderates the climate. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Peninsula" title="Scandinavian Peninsula">Scandinavian Peninsula</a> has more moderate climate than similarly northern latitudes of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Canada" title="Northern Canada">northern Canada</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> enables this moderating effect. The windward side of a land mass experiences more moderation than the leeward side. In the Northern Hemisphere, the prevailing wind is west-to-east, and western coasts tend to be milder than eastern coasts. This is seen in Eastern North America and Western Europe, where rough continental climates appear on the east coast on parallels with mild climates on the other side of the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[157]</a></sup> In the Southern Hemisphere, the prevailing wind is east-to-west, and the eastern coasts are milder.</li>
<li>The distance from the Earth to the Sun varies. The Earth is closest to the Sun (at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>) in January, which is summer in the Southern Hemisphere. It is furthest away (at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a>) in July, which is summer in the Northern Hemisphere, and only 93.55% of the solar radiation from the Sun falls on a given square area of land than at perihelion. Despite this, there are larger land masses in the Northern Hemisphere, which are easier to heat than the seas. Consequently, summers are 2.3 °C (4 °F) warmer in the Northern Hemisphere than in the Southern Hemisphere under similar conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earth_at_Aphelion_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Earth_at_Aphelion-175">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li>The climate is colder at high altitudes than at sea level because of the decreased air density.</li>
</ul>
<p>The commonly used <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> system has five broad groups (<a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">humid tropics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">arid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid middle latitudes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental</a> and cold <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">polar</a>), which are further divided into more specific subtypes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berger2002_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-berger2002-169">[152]</a></sup> The Köppen system rates regions of terrain based on observed temperature and precipitation.</p>
<p>The highest air temperature ever measured on Earth was 56.7 °C (134.1 °F) in <a href="/wiki/Furnace_Creek,_California" title="Furnace Creek, California">Furnace Creek, California</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Death_Valley_National_Park" title="Death Valley National Park">Death Valley</a>, in 1913.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[159]</a></sup> The lowest air temperature ever directly measured on Earth was −89.2 °C (−128.6 °F) at <a href="/wiki/Vostok_Station" title="Vostok Station">Vostok Station</a> in 1983,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[160]</a></sup> but satellites have used remote sensing to measure temperatures as low as −94.7 °C (−138.5 °F) in <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctica" title="East Antarctica">East Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[161]</a></sup> These temperature records are only measurements made with modern instruments from the 20th century onwards and likely do not reflect the full range of temperature on Earth.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_atmosphere">Upper atmosphere</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2043" data-file-width="3064" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg/220px-Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg/330px-Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg/440px-Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Full_moon_partially_obscured_by_atmosphere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This view from orbit shows the <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">full Moon</a> partially obscured by Earth's atmosphere.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Above the troposphere, the atmosphere is usually divided into the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesosphere" title="Mesosphere">mesosphere</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atmosphere_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-atmosphere-163">[146]</a></sup> Each layer has a different <a href="/wiki/Lapse_rate" title="Lapse rate">lapse rate</a>, defining the rate of change in temperature with height. Beyond these, the <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a> thins out into the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, where the geomagnetic fields interact with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciweek2004_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciweek2004-179">[162]</a></sup> Within the stratosphere is the ozone layer, a component that partially shields the surface from ultraviolet light and thus is important for life on Earth. The <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="Kármán line">Kármán line</a>, defined as 100 km above Earth's surface, is a working definition for the boundary between the atmosphere and <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cordoba2004_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-cordoba2004-180">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thermal energy causes some of the molecules at the outer edge of the atmosphere to increase their velocity to the point where they can escape from Earth's gravity. This causes a slow but steady <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">loss of the atmosphere into space</a>. Because unfixed <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> has a low <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular mass</a>, it can achieve <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> more readily, and it leaks into outer space at a greater rate than other gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas31_4_1118_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-jas31_4_1118-181">[164]</a></sup> The leakage of hydrogen into space contributes to the shifting of Earth's atmosphere and surface from an initially <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> state to its current <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidizing</a> one. Photosynthesis provided a source of free oxygen, but the loss of reducing agents such as hydrogen is thought to have been a necessary precondition for the widespread accumulation of oxygen in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci293_5531_839_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci293_5531_839-182">[165]</a></sup> Hence the ability of hydrogen to escape from the atmosphere may have influenced the nature of life that developed on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abedon1997_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-abedon1997-183">[166]</a></sup> In the current, oxygen-rich atmosphere most hydrogen is converted into water before it has an opportunity to escape. Instead, most of the hydrogen loss comes from the destruction of methane in the upper atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arwps4_265_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-arwps4_265-184">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_field">Gravitational field</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">Gravity of Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geoids_sm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="350" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Geoids_sm.jpg/220px-Geoids_sm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Geoids_sm.jpg/330px-Geoids_sm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Geoids_sm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geoids_sm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Earth's gravity measured by NASA's <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Climate_Experiment" title="Gravity Recovery and Climate Experiment">GRACE</a> mission, showing deviations from the <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_gravity" title="Theoretical gravity">theoretical gravity</a>. Red shows where gravity is stronger than the smooth, standard value, and blue shows where it is weaker.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">gravity of Earth</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> that is imparted to objects due to the distribution of mass within the Earth. Near the Earth's surface, <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration" title="Gravitational acceleration">gravitational acceleration</a> is approximately 9.8 m/s<sup>2</sup> (32 ft/s<sup>2</sup>). Local differences in <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, and deeper tectonic structure cause local and broad, regional differences in the Earth's gravitational field, known as <a href="/wiki/Gravity_anomaly" title="Gravity anomaly">gravitational anomalies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field">Magnetic field</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a></div>
<p>The main part of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> is generated in the core, the site of a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">dynamo</a> process that converts the kinetic energy of thermally and compositionally driven convection into electrical and magnetic field energy. The field extends outwards from the core, through the mantle, and up to Earth's surface, where it is, approximately, a <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a>. The poles of the dipole are located close to Earth's geographic poles. At the equator of the magnetic field, the magnetic-field strength at the surface is <span class="nowrap">3.05 × 10<sup>−5</sup> <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">T</a></span>, with global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Magnetic dipole moment">magnetic dipole moment</a> of <span class="nowrap">7.91 × 10<sup>15</sup> T m<sup>3</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang2003_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang2003-186">[169]</a></sup> The convection movements in the core are chaotic; the magnetic poles drift and periodically change alignment. This causes <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_secular_variation" title="Geomagnetic secular variation">secular variation</a> of the main field and <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">field reversals</a> at irregular intervals averaging a few times every million years. The most recent reversal occurred approximately 700,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitzpatrick2006_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-fitzpatrick2006-187">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbelwh_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbelwh-188">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg"><img alt="Diagram showing the magnetic field lines of Earth's magnetosphere. The lines are swept back in the anti-solar direction under the influence of the solar wind." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="512" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg/220px-Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg/330px-Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg/440px-Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Structure_of_the_magnetosphere-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematic of Earth's magnetosphere. The solar wind flows from left to right</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The extent of Earth's magnetic field in space defines the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>. Ions and electrons of the solar wind are deflected by the magnetosphere; solar wind pressure compresses the dayside of the magnetosphere, to about 10 Earth radii, and extends the nightside magnetosphere into a long tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-189">[172]</a></sup> Because the velocity of the solar wind is greater than the speed at which wave propagate through the solar wind, a supersonic bowshock precedes the dayside magnetosphere within the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[173]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">Charged particles</a> are contained within the magnetosphere; the plasmasphere is defined by low-energy particles that essentially follow magnetic field lines as Earth rotates;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[175]</a></sup> the ring current is defined by medium-energy particles that drift relative to the geomagnetic field, but with paths that are still dominated by the magnetic field,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaumjohannTreumann1997_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaumjohannTreumann1997-193">[176]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt" title="Van Allen radiation belt">Van Allen radiation belt</a> are formed by high-energy particles whose motion is essentially random, but otherwise contained by the magnetosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-189">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Allen_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Allen-194">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_storm" title="Magnetic storm">magnetic storms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Substorm" title="Substorm">substorms</a>, charged particles can be deflected from the outer magnetosphere and especially the magnetotail, directed along field lines into Earth's ionosphere, where atmospheric atoms can be excited and ionized, causing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern2005_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern2005-195">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation">Rotation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">Earth's rotation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EpicEarth-Globespin(2016May29).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/EpicEarth-Globespin%282016May29%29.gif/220px-EpicEarth-Globespin%282016May29%29.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/EpicEarth-Globespin%282016May29%29.gif/330px-EpicEarth-Globespin%282016May29%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/EpicEarth-Globespin%282016May29%29.gif/440px-EpicEarth-Globespin%282016May29%29.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EpicEarth-Globespin(2016May29).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Earth's rotation imaged by <a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Climate_Observatory" title="Deep Space Climate Observatory">DSCOVR EPIC</a> on 29 May 2016, a few weeks before the solstice</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth's rotation period relative to the Sun—its mean solar day—is <span class="nowrap">86,400 seconds</span> of mean solar time (<span class="nowrap">86,400.0025 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a> seconds</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aj136_5_1906_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-aj136_5_1906-196">[179]</a></sup> Because Earth's solar day is now slightly longer than it was during the 19th century due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal deceleration</a>, each day varies between <span class="nowrap">0 and 2 SI <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">ms</a></span> longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNO_TSD_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-USNO_TSD-197">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Earth's rotation period relative to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_star" title="Fixed star">fixed stars</a>, called its <i>stellar day</i> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Earth_Rotation_and_Reference_Systems_Service" title="International Earth Rotation and Reference Systems Service">International Earth Rotation and Reference Systems Service</a> (IERS), is <span class="nowrap">86,164.0989 seconds</span> of mean solar time (UT1), or <span class="nowrap">23<sup><small>h</small></sup> 56<sup><small>m</small></sup> 4.0989<sup><small>s</small></sup>.</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IERS_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-IERS-4">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aoki_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aoki-199">[n 18]</a></sup> Earth's rotation period relative to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precession_(astronomy)" title="Precession (astronomy)">precessing</a> or moving mean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernal_equinox" title="Vernal equinox">vernal equinox</a>, misnamed its <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">sidereal day</a></i>, is <span class="nowrap">86,164.0905 seconds</span> of mean solar time (UT1) <span class="nowrap">(23<sup><small>h</small></sup> 56<sup><small>m</small></sup> 4.0905<sup><small>s</small></sup>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IERS_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-IERS-4">[2]</a></sup> Thus the sidereal day is shorter than the stellar day by about 8.4 ms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seidelmann1992_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-seidelmann1992-200">[182]</a></sup> The length of the mean solar day in SI seconds is available from the IERS for the periods 1623–2005<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iers1623_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-iers1623-201">[183]</a></sup> and 1962–2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iers1962_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-iers1962-202">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from meteors within the atmosphere and low-orbiting satellites, the main apparent motion of celestial bodies in Earth's sky is to the west at a rate of 15°/h = 15'/min. For bodies near the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a>, this is equivalent to an apparent diameter of the Sun or the Moon every two minutes; from Earth's surface, the apparent sizes of the Sun and the Moon are approximately the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeilik1998_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik1998-203">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angular_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-angular-204">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">Orbit</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">Earth's orbit</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pale_Blue_Dot.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="453" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pale_Blue_Dot.png/170px-Pale_Blue_Dot.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pale_Blue_Dot.png/255px-Pale_Blue_Dot.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pale_Blue_Dot.png/340px-Pale_Blue_Dot.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pale_Blue_Dot.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Pale_Blue_Dot" title="Pale Blue Dot">Pale Blue Dot</a></i> photo taken in 1990 by the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> spacecraft showing Earth (center right) from nearly 6.4 billion km (4 billion mi) away</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth orbits the Sun at an average distance of about 150 million km (93 million mi) every 365.2564 mean solar days, or one <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_year" title="Sidereal year">sidereal year</a>. This gives an apparent movement of the Sun eastward with respect to the stars at a rate of about 1°/day, which is one apparent Sun or Moon diameter every 12 hours. Due to this motion, on average it takes 24 hours—a <a href="/wiki/Solar_time" title="Solar time">solar day</a>—for Earth to complete a full rotation about its axis so that the Sun returns to the <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(astronomy)" title="Meridian (astronomy)">meridian</a>. The orbital speed of Earth averages about 29.78 km/s (107,200 km/h; 66,600 mph), which is fast enough to travel a distance equal to Earth's diameter, about 12,742 km (7,918 mi), in seven minutes, and the distance to the Moon, 384,000 km (239,000 mi), in about 3.5 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earth_fact_sheet_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Moon and Earth orbit a common <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> every 27.32 days relative to the background stars. When combined with the Earth–Moon system's common orbit around the Sun, the period of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_month" title="Synodic month">synodic month</a>, from new moon to new moon, is 29.53 days. Viewed from the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_pole" title="Celestial pole">celestial north pole</a>, the motion of Earth, the Moon, and their axial rotations are all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterclockwise" title="Counterclockwise">counterclockwise</a>. Viewed from a vantage point above the north poles of both the Sun and Earth, Earth orbits in a counterclockwise direction about the Sun. The orbital and axial planes are not precisely aligned: Earth's <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axis is tilted</a> some 23.44 degrees from the perpendicular to the Earth–Sun plane (the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>), and the Earth–Moon plane is tilted up to ±5.1 degrees against the Earth–Sun plane. Without this tilt, there would be an eclipse every two weeks, alternating between <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earth_fact_sheet_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-earth_fact_sheet-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moon_fact_sheet_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-moon_fact_sheet-205">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a>, or the sphere of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> influence, of the Earth is about 1.5 million kilometres (930,000 mi) in radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vazquez_etal2006_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-vazquez_etal2006-206">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_radius_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-hill_radius-207">[n 19]</a></sup> This is the maximum distance at which the Earth's gravitational influence is stronger than the more distant Sun and planets. Objects must orbit the Earth within this radius, or they can become unbound by the gravitational perturbation of the Sun.</p>
<p>Earth, along with the Solar System, is situated in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> and orbits about 28,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> from its center. It is about 20 light-years above the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20051201_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20051201-208">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Axial_tilt_and_seasons">Axial tilt and seasons</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt#Earth" title="Axial tilt">Axial tilt § Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AxialTiltObliquity.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="760" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/AxialTiltObliquity.png/220px-AxialTiltObliquity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/AxialTiltObliquity.png/330px-AxialTiltObliquity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/AxialTiltObliquity.png/440px-AxialTiltObliquity.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AxialTiltObliquity.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Earth's axial tilt (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obliquity" title="Obliquity">obliquity</a>) and its relation to the <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotation axis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">plane of orbit</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The axial tilt of the Earth is approximately 23.439281°<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IERS_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-IERS-4">[2]</a></sup> with the axis of its orbit plane, always pointing towards the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_Poles" title="Celestial Poles">Celestial Poles</a>. Due to Earth's axial tilt, the amount of sunlight reaching any given point on the surface varies over the course of the year. This causes the seasonal change in climate, with <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> occurring when the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a> is facing the Sun, and <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a> taking place when the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn">Tropic of Capricorn</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> faces the Sun. During the summer, the day lasts longer, and the Sun climbs higher in the sky. In winter, the climate becomes cooler and the days shorter. In northern temperate latitudes, the Sun rises north of true east during the summer solstice, and sets north of true west, reversing in the winter. The Sun rises south of true east in the summer for the southern temperate zone and sets south of true west.</p>
<p>Above the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, an extreme case is reached where there is no daylight at all for part of the year, up to six months at the North Pole itself, a <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">polar night</a>. In the Southern Hemisphere, the situation is exactly reversed, with the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a> oriented opposite the direction of the North Pole. Six months later, this pole will experience a <a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">midnight sun</a>, a day of 24 hours, again reversing with the South Pole.</p>
<p>By astronomical convention, the four seasons can be determined by the <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstices</a>—the points in the orbit of maximum axial tilt toward or away from the Sun—and the <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinoxes</a>, when the direction of the tilt and the direction to the Sun are perpendicular. In the Northern Hemisphere, <a href="/wiki/Winter_solstice" title="Winter solstice">winter solstice</a> currently occurs around 21 December; <a href="/wiki/Summer_solstice" title="Summer solstice">summer solstice</a> is near 21 June, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_equinox" title="Spring equinox">spring equinox</a> is around 20 March and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autumnal_equinox" title="Autumnal equinox">autumnal equinox</a> is about 22 or 23 September. In the Southern Hemisphere, the situation is reversed, with the summer and winter solstices exchanged and the spring and autumnal equinox dates swapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bromberg2008_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-bromberg2008-209">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>The angle of Earth's axial tilt is relatively stable over long periods of time. Its axial tilt does undergo <a href="/wiki/Nutation" title="Nutation">nutation</a>; a slight, irregular motion with a main period of 18.6 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin2006_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-lin2006-210">[191]</a></sup> The orientation (rather than the angle) of Earth's axis also changes over time, <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precessing</a> around in a complete circle over each 25,800 year cycle; this precession is the reason for the difference between a sidereal year and a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_year" title="Tropical year">tropical year</a>. Both of these motions are caused by the varying attraction of the Sun and the Moon on Earth's equatorial bulge. The poles also migrate a few meters across Earth's surface. This <a href="/wiki/Polar_motion" title="Polar motion">polar motion</a> has multiple, cyclical components, which collectively are termed <a href="/wiki/Quasiperiodic_motion" title="Quasiperiodic motion">quasiperiodic motion</a>. In addition to an annual component to this motion, there is a 14-month cycle called the <a href="/wiki/Chandler_wobble" title="Chandler wobble">Chandler wobble</a>. Earth's rotational velocity also varies in a phenomenon known as length-of-day variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fisher19960205_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-fisher19960205-211">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>In modern times, Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> occurs around 3 January, and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> around 4 July. These dates change over time due to precession and other orbital factors, which follow cyclical patterns known as <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a>. The changing Earth–Sun distance causes an increase of about 6.9%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_energy_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-solar_energy-212">[n 20]</a></sup> in solar energy reaching Earth at perihelion relative to aphelion. Because the Southern Hemisphere is tilted toward the Sun at about the same time that Earth reaches the closest approach to the Sun, the Southern Hemisphere receives slightly more energy from the Sun than does the northern over the course of a year. This effect is much less significant than the total energy change due to the axial tilt, and most of the excess energy is absorbed by the higher proportion of water in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams20051230_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams20051230-213">[193]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitability">Habitability</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg/220px-Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg/330px-Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg/440px-Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moraine_Lake_17092005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> in Canada overlook <a href="/wiki/Moraine_Lake" title="Moraine Lake">Moraine Lake</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A planet that can sustain life is termed <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a>, even if life did not originate there. Earth provides liquid water—an environment where complex <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic molecules</a> can assemble and interact, and sufficient energy to sustain <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ab2003_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-ab2003-214">[194]</a></sup> The distance of Earth from the Sun, as well as its orbital eccentricity, rate of rotation, axial tilt, geological history, sustaining atmosphere, and magnetic field all contribute to the current climatic conditions at the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dole1970_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-dole1970-215">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosphere">Biosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">Biosphere</a></div>
<p>A planet's life forms inhabit <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, whose total is sometimes said to form a "biosphere". Earth's biosphere is thought to have begun <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolving</a> about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000350000000000000♠</span>3.5 Gya</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-83">[71]</a></sup> The biosphere is divided into a number of <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a>, inhabited by broadly similar plants and animals. On land, biomes are separated primarily by differences in latitude, <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">height above sea level</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>. Terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">biomes</a> lying within the Arctic or <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circles</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">high altitudes</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">extremely arid areas</a> are relatively barren of plant and animal life; <a href="/wiki/Latitudinal_gradients_in_species_diversity" title="Latitudinal gradients in species diversity">species diversity</a> reaches a peak in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">humid lowlands at equatorial latitudes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnat163_2_192_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-amnat163_2_192-216">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2016, scientists reported identifying a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Universal_Common_Ancestor" title="Last Universal Common Ancestor">Last Universal Common Ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> living on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160725_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160725-217">[197]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_resources_and_land_use">Natural resources and land use</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">Natural resource</a> and <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">Land use</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:1em;">
<caption>Estimated human land use, 2000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambin2011_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lambin2011-218">[198]</a></sup></caption>
<tr>
<th>Land use</th>
<th>Mha</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Cropland</td>
<td style="text-align:center">1,510–1,611</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Pastures</td>
<td style="text-align:center">2,500–3,410</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Natural forests</td>
<td style="text-align:center">3,143–3,871</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Planted forests</td>
<td style="text-align:center">126–215</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Urban areas</td>
<td style="text-align:center">66–351</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Unused, productive land</td>
<td style="text-align:center">356–445</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Earth has resources that have been exploited by humans. Those termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resources" title="Non-renewable resources">non-renewable resources</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, only renew over geological timescales.</p>
<p>Large deposits of fossil fuels are obtained from Earth's crust, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. These deposits are used by humans both for energy production and as feedstock for chemical production. Mineral <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> bodies have also been formed within the crust through a process of <a href="/wiki/Ore_genesis" title="Ore genesis">ore genesis</a>, resulting from actions of <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magmatism</a>, erosion, and plate tectonics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramdohr_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramdohr-219">[199]</a></sup> These bodies form concentrated sources for many metals and other useful <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a>.</p>
<p>Earth's biosphere produces many useful biological products for humans, including food, <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceuticals</a>, oxygen, and the recycling of many organic wastes. The land-based <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> depends upon <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a> and fresh water, and the oceanic ecosystem depends upon dissolved nutrients washed down from the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science299_5607_673_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-science299_5607_673-220">[200]</a></sup> In 1980, 5,053 <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">Mha</a> (50.53 million km<sup>2</sup>) of Earth's land surface consisted of forest and woodlands, 6,788 Mha (67.88 million km<sup>2</sup>) was grasslands and pasture, and 1,501 Mha (15.01 million km<sup>2</sup>) was cultivated as croplands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner1990_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner1990-221">[201]</a></sup> The estimated amount of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrigated_land" title="Irrigated land">irrigated land</a> in 1993 was 2,481,250 square kilometres (958,020 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia-17">[14]</a></sup> Humans also live on the land by using <a href="/wiki/Building_material" title="Building material">building materials</a> to construct shelters.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_and_environmental_hazards">Natural and environmental hazards</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pavlof2014iss.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1440" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pavlof2014iss.jpg/220px-Pavlof2014iss.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pavlof2014iss.jpg/330px-Pavlof2014iss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pavlof2014iss.jpg/440px-Pavlof2014iss.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pavlof2014iss.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A volcano injecting hot ash into the atmosphere</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Large areas of Earth's surface are subject to extreme weather such as tropical <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricanes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Typhoon" title="Typhoon">typhoons</a> that dominate life in those areas. From 1980 to 2000, these events caused an average of 11,800 human deaths per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walsh2008_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-walsh2008-222">[202]</a></sup> Many places are subject to earthquakes, <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic eruptions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkholes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blizzard" title="Blizzard">blizzards</a>, floods, droughts, <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>, and other calamities and disasters.</p>
<p>Many localized areas are subject to human-made <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> of the air and water, <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> and toxic substances, loss of vegetation (<a href="/wiki/Overgrazing" title="Overgrazing">overgrazing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>), loss of wildlife, species <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" title="Soil degradation">soil degradation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_depletion" title="Soil depletion">soil depletion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>.</p>
<p>There is a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> linking human activities to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> due to industrial carbon dioxide emissions. This is predicted to produce changes such as the melting of glaciers and ice sheets, more extreme temperature ranges, significant changes in weather and a <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">global rise in average sea levels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un20070202_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-un20070202-223">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_geography">Human geography</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_geography" title="Human geography">Human geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">World</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Continents_vide_couleurs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="1500" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Continents_vide_couleurs.png/300px-Continents_vide_couleurs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Continents_vide_couleurs.png/450px-Continents_vide_couleurs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Continents_vide_couleurs.png/600px-Continents_vide_couleurs.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
