</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">Cartography</a>, the study and practice of map-making, and <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, the study of the lands, features, inhabitants and phenomena on Earth, have historically been the disciplines devoted to depicting Earth. <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">Surveying</a>, the determination of locations and distances, and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a>, the determination of position and direction, have developed alongside cartography and geography, providing and suitably quantifying the requisite information.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">Earth's human population</a> reached approximately seven billion on 31 October 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[205]</a></sup> Projections indicate that the world's human population will reach 9.2 billion in 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un2006_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-un2006-226">[206]</a></sup> Most of the growth is expected to take place in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nations" title="Developing nations">developing nations</a>. <a href="/wiki/Population_density#Human_population_density" title="Population density">Human population density</a> varies widely around the world, but a majority live in Asia. By 2020, 60% of the world's population is expected to be living in urban, rather than rural, areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prb2007_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-prb2007-227">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is estimated that one-eighth of Earth's surface is suitable for humans to live on – three-quarters of Earth's surface is covered by oceans, leaving one-quarter as land. Half of that land area is desert (14%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hessd4_439_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-hessd4_439-228">[208]</a></sup> high mountains (27%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiv_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-biodiv-229">[209]</a></sup> or other unsuitable terrains. The northernmost permanent settlement in the world is <a href="/wiki/Alert,_Nunavut" title="Alert, Nunavut">Alert</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Ellesmere_Island" title="Ellesmere Island">Ellesmere Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfsa2006_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfsa2006-230">[210]</a></sup> (82°28′N) The southernmost is the <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station</a>, in Antarctica, almost exactly at the South Pole. (90°S)</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_(8020913157).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_%288020913157%29.jpg/220px-67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_%288020913157%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_%288020913157%29.jpg/330px-67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_%288020913157%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_%288020913157%29.jpg/440px-67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_%288020913157%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:67%C2%BA_Per%C3%ADodo_de_Sesiones_de_la_Asamblea_General_de_Naciones_Unidas_(8020913157).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Headquarters_of_the_United_Nations" title="Headquarters of the United Nations">Headquarters of the United Nations</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Independent sovereign nations claim the planet's entire land surface, except for some parts of Antarctica, a few <a href="/wiki/Croatia%E2%80%93Serbia_border_dispute" title="Croatia–Serbia border dispute">land parcels along the Danube</a> river's western bank, and the <a href="/wiki/Terra_nullius" title="Terra nullius">unclaimed area</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bir_Tawil" title="Bir Tawil">Bir Tawil</a> between Egypt and Sudan. As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Earth&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 193 <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states" title="List of sovereign states">sovereign states</a> that are <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_United_Nations" title="Member states of the United Nations">member states of the United Nations</a>, plus two <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_observers" title="United Nations General Assembly observers">observer states</a> and 72 <a href="/wiki/Dependent_territory" title="Dependent territory">dependent territories</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_limited_recognition" title="List of states with limited recognition">states with limited recognition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia-17">[14]</a></sup> Earth has never had a <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> government with authority over the entire globe, although some nation-states have striven for <a href="/wiki/Hyperpower" title="Hyperpower">world domination</a> and failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy1989_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy1989-231">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> is a worldwide <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">intergovernmental organization</a> that was created with the goal of intervening in the disputes between nations, thereby avoiding armed conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uncharter_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-uncharter-232">[212]</a></sup> The U.N. serves primarily as a forum for international diplomacy and <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>. When the consensus of the membership permits, it provides a mechanism for armed intervention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un_int_law_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-un_int_law-233">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first human to orbit Earth was <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> on 12 April 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kuhn2006_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-kuhn2006-234">[214]</a></sup> In total, about 487 people have visited outer space and reached orbit as of 30 July 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Earth&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, and, of these, <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">twelve</a> have walked on the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ellis2004_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-ellis2004-235">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shayler_vis2005_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-shayler_vis2005-236">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wade2008_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-wade2008-237">[217]</a></sup> Normally, the only humans in space are those on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>. The station's <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions" title="List of International Space Station expeditions">crew</a>, made up of six people, is usually replaced every six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_rg_iss2007_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_rg_iss2007-238">[218]</a></sup> The farthest that humans have traveled from Earth is 400,171 km, achieved during the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> mission in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apollo13History_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo13History-239">[219]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon">Moon</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 0.5em;">
<caption>Characteristics</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FullMoon2010.jpg" title="Full moon as seen from Earth's Northern Hemisphere"><img alt="Full moon as seen from Earth's Northern Hemisphere" data-file-height="2452" data-file-width="2580" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/FullMoon2010.jpg/200px-FullMoon2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/FullMoon2010.jpg/300px-FullMoon2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/FullMoon2010.jpg/400px-FullMoon2010.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Diameter</b></td>
<td>3,474.8 km</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Mass</b></td>
<td>7.349<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">22</span></sup> kg</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">Semi-major axis</a></b></td>
<td>384,400 km</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Orbital period</b></td>
<td><span class="nowrap">27 d 7 h 43.7 m</span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a></div>
<p>The Moon is a relatively large, <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial</a>, planet-like <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellite</a>, with a diameter about one-quarter of Earth's. It is the largest moon in the Solar System relative to the size of its planet, although <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> is larger relative to the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>. The natural satellites of other planets are also referred to as "moons", after Earth's.</p>
<p>The gravitational attraction between Earth and the Moon causes <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> on Earth. The same effect on the Moon has led to its <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidal locking</a>: its rotation period is the same as the time it takes to orbit Earth. As a result, it always presents the same face to the planet. As the Moon orbits Earth, different parts of its face are illuminated by the Sun, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">lunar phases</a>; the dark part of the face is separated from the light part by the <a href="/wiki/Terminator_(solar)" title="Terminator (solar)">solar terminator</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth-Moon.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="512" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Earth-Moon.svg/220px-Earth-Moon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Earth-Moon.svg/330px-Earth-Moon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Earth-Moon.svg/440px-Earth-Moon.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth-Moon.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Details of the Earth–Moon system, showing the radius of each object and the Earth–Moon <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a>. The Moon's axis is located by <a href="/wiki/Cassini%27s_laws" title="Cassini's laws">Cassini's third law</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to their <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal interaction</a>, the Moon recedes from Earth at the rate of approximately 38 mm/yr. Over millions of years, these tiny modifications—and the lengthening of Earth's day by about 23 <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">µs</a>/yr—add up to significant changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espenak_meeus20070207_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-espenak_meeus20070207-240">[220]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period, for example, (approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002410000000000000♠</span>410 Mya</span>) there were 400 days in a year, with each day lasting 21.8 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Moon may have dramatically affected the development of life by moderating the planet's climate. <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">Paleontological</a> evidence and computer simulations show that Earth's axial tilt is stabilized by tidal interactions with the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa428_261_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-aaa428_261-33">[28]</a></sup> Some theorists think that without this stabilization against the <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torques</a> applied by the Sun and planets to Earth's equatorial bulge, the rotational axis might be chaotically unstable, exhibiting chaotic changes over millions of years, as appears to be the case for Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature410_6830_773_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature410_6830_773-242">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Viewed from Earth, the Moon is just far enough away to have almost the same apparent-sized disk as the Sun. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_size" title="Angular size">angular size</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Solid_angle" title="Solid angle">solid angle</a>) of these two bodies match because, although the Sun's diameter is about 400 times as large as the Moon's, it is also 400 times more distant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angular_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-angular-204">[186]</a></sup> This allows total and annular solar eclipses to occur on Earth.</p>
<p>The most widely accepted theory of the Moon's origin, the <a href="/wiki/Giant-impact_hypothesis" title="Giant-impact hypothesis">giant-impact hypothesis</a>, states that it formed from the collision of a Mars-size protoplanet called Theia with the early Earth. This hypothesis explains (among other things) the Moon's relative lack of iron and volatile elements and the fact that its composition is nearly identical to that of Earth's crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature412_708_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature412_708-243">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroids_and_artificial_satellites">Asteroids and artificial satellites</span></h2>
<p>Earth has at least five <a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">co-orbital asteroids</a>, including <a href="/wiki/3753_Cruithne" title="3753 Cruithne">3753 Cruithne</a> and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2002_AA29" title="2002 AA29">2002 AA<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">29</span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitehouse20021021_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-whitehouse20021021-244">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christou_asher2011_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-christou_asher2011-245">[225]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Earth_trojan" title="Earth trojan">trojan asteroid</a> companion, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2010_TK7" title="2010 TK7">2010 TK<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">7</span></span></a></span>, is librating around the leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagrange_point" title="Lagrange point">Lagrange triangular point</a>, L4, in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">Earth's orbit</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Connors_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Connors-246">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi-247">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tiny <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroid</a> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2006_RH120" title="2006 RH120">2006 RH<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">120</span></span></a></span> makes close approaches to the Earth–Moon system roughly every twenty years. During these approaches, it can orbit Earth for brief periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of June 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Earth&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 1,419 operational, human-made <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a> orbiting Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucs_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ucs-7">[5]</a></sup> There are also inoperative satellites, including <a href="/wiki/Vanguard_1" title="Vanguard 1">Vanguard 1</a>, the oldest satellite currently in orbit, and over 16,000 pieces of tracked <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_debris_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-space_debris-8">[n 3]</a></sup> Earth's largest artificial satellite is the International Space Station.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_and_historical_viewpoint">Cultural and historical viewpoint</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth_in_culture" title="Earth in culture">Earth in culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg/220px-NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg/330px-NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg/440px-NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Earthrise" title="Earthrise">Earthrise</a></i>, taken by astronauts on board <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The standard astronomical symbol of Earth consists of a cross <a href="/wiki/Circumscribed_circle" title="Circumscribed circle">circumscribed by a circle</a>, <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_symbol.svg"><img alt="Earth symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Earth_symbol.svg/18px-Earth_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Earth_symbol.svg/27px-Earth_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Earth_symbol.svg/36px-Earth_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="18"/></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liungman2004_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-liungman2004-249">[229]</a></sup> representing the <a href="/wiki/Four_corners_of_the_world" title="Four corners of the world">four corners of the world</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">Human cultures</a> have developed many views of the planet. Earth is sometimes <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">personified</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>. In many cultures it is a <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddess</a> that is also the primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_deity" title="Fertility deity">fertility deity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-250">[230]</a></sup> and by the mid-20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia Principle</a> compared Earth's environments and life as a single self-regulating organism leading to broad stabilization of the conditions of habitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanishing255_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanishing255-251">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J1972_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-J1972-252">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lovelock1974_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-lovelock1974-253">[233]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">Creation myths</a> in many religions involve the creation of Earth by a supernatural <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> or deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-250">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scientific investigation has resulted in several culturally transformative shifts in people's view of the planet. In the West, belief in a <a href="/wiki/Flat_Earth" title="Flat Earth">flat Earth</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell1997_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell1997-254">[234]</a></sup> was displaced by the idea of <a href="/wiki/Spherical_Earth" title="Spherical Earth">spherical Earth</a>, credited to <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> in the 6th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[235]</a></sup> Earth was further believed to be <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">the center of the universe</a> until the 16th century when scientists first theorized that it was <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">a moving object</a>, comparable to the other planets in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arnett20060716_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-arnett20060716-256">[236]</a></sup> Due to the efforts of influential Christian scholars and clerics such as <a href="/wiki/James_Ussher" title="James Ussher">James Ussher</a>, who sought to determine the age of Earth through analysis of genealogies in Scripture, Westerners before the 19th century generally believed Earth to be a few thousand years old at most. It was only during the 19th century that geologists realized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_age" title="Earth's age">Earth's age</a> was at least many millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a> used <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a> to estimate the age of Earth to be between 20 million and 400 million years in 1864, sparking a vigorous debate on the subject; it was only when radioactivity and <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radioactive dating</a> were discovered in the late 19th and early 20th centuries that a reliable mechanism for determining Earth's age was established, proving the planet to be billions of years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[239]</a></sup> The perception of Earth shifted again in the 20th century when humans first viewed it from orbit, and especially with photographs of Earth returned by the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[240]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-epoch-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-epoch_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All astronomical quantities vary, both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular_phenomena" title="Secular phenomena">secularly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">periodically</a>. The quantities given are the values at the instant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J2000.0" title="J2000.0">J2000.0</a> of the secular variation, ignoring all periodic variations.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-apsis-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-apsis_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-apsis_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">aphelion = <i>a</i> × (1 + <i>e</i>); perihelion = <i>a</i> × (1 – <i>e</i>), where <i>a</i> is the semi-major axis and <i>e</i> is the eccentricity. The difference between Earth's perihelion and aphelion is 5 million kilometers.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-space_debris-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-space_debris_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-space_debris_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">As of 5 July 2016, the United States Strategic Command tracked a total of 17,729 artificial objects, mostly debris. See: <cite class="citation magazine"><a class="external text" href="https://orbitaldebris.jsc.nasa.gov/quarterly-news/pdfs/odqnv20i3.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Orbital Debris Quarterly News"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. Vol. 20 no. 3. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>. July 2016. p. 8<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 October</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEarth&amp;rft.btitle=Orbital+Debris+Quarterly+News&amp;rft.date=2016-07&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pages=8&amp;rft.pub=NASA&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Forbitaldebris.jsc.nasa.gov%2Fquarterly-news%2Fpdfs%2Fodqnv20i3.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-surfacecover-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-surfacecover_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Due to natural fluctuations, ambiguities surrounding <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">ice shelves</a>, and mapping conventions for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertical_datum" title="Vertical datum">vertical datums</a>, exact values for land and ocean coverage are not meaningful. Based on data from the <a href="/wiki/Vector_Map" title="Vector Map">Vector Map</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.landcover.org/" rel="nofollow">Global Landcover</a> datasets, extreme values for coverage of lakes and streams are 0.6% and 1.0% of Earth's surface. The ice shields of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> are counted as land, even though much of the rock that supports them lies below sea level.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-sidereal_solar-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-sidereal_solar_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The number of solar days is one less than the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">sidereal days</a> because the orbital motion of Earth around the Sun causes one additional revolution of the planet about its axis.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Including <i>eorþe</i>, <i>erþe</i>, <i>erde</i>, and <i>erthe</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As in <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i> (1531–33):<br/>
<i>Wearp ða wundelmæl   wrættum gebunden<br/>
yrre oretta,   þæt hit on <b>eorðan</b> læg,<br/>
stið ond stylecg.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beo_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-beo-46">[40]</a></sup><br/>
"He threw the artfully-wound sword so that it lay upon the <b>earth</b>, firm and sharp-edged."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beo_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-beo-46">[40]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As in the Old English glosses of the <i><a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Luke_13" title="Luke 13">Luke 13</a>:7):<br/>
Succidite ergo illam ut quid etiam <b>terram</b> occupat: <i>hrendas</i> uel <i>scearfað forðon ðailca</i> uel <i>hia to huon uutedlice <b>eorðo</b> gionetað</i> uel <i>gemerras.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup><br/>
"Remove it. Why should it use up the <b>soil</b>?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[41]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As in <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfric_of_Eynsham" title="Ælfric of Eynsham">Ælfric</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Heptateuch" title="Heptateuch">Heptateuch</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Gen. 1</a>:10):<br/>
<i>Ond God gecygde ða drignysse <b>eorðan</b> ond ðære wætera gegaderunge he het sæ</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[42]</a></sup><br/>
"And God called the dry land <b>Earth</b>; and the gathering together of the waters called he Seas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[43]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As in the <a href="/wiki/Wessex_Gospels" title="Wessex Gospels">Wessex Gospels</a> (<a href="/wiki/Matthew_28" title="Matthew 28">Matt. 28</a>:18):<br/>
<i>Me is geseald ælc anweald on heofonan &amp; on <b>eorðan</b></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup><br/>
"All authority in heaven and on <b>earth</b> has been given to me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[44]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codex_Junius" title="Codex Junius">Codex Junius</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Genesis_A" title="Genesis A">Genesis</a></i> (112–16):<br/>
<i>her ærest gesceop   ece drihten,<br/>
helm eallwihta,   heofon and <b>eorðan</b>,<br/>
rodor arærde   and þis rume land<br/>
gestaþelode   strangum mihtum,<br/>
frea ælmihtig.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[45]</a></sup><br/>
"Here first with mighty power the Everlasting Lord, the Helm of all created things, Almighty King, made <b>earth</b> and heaven, raised up the sky and founded the spacious land."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[46]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As in <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfric_of_Eynsham" title="Ælfric of Eynsham">Ælfric</a>'s <i>On the Seasons of the Year</i> <span class="nowrap">(Ch. 6,</span> §9):<br/>
<i>Seo <b>eorðe</b> stent on gelicnysse anre pinnhnyte, &amp; seo sunne glit onbutan be Godes gesetnysse.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedearth_44-7"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-44">[39]</a></sup><br/>
"The <b>earth</b> can be compared to a pine cone, and the Sun glides around it by God's decree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[47]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">If Earth were shrunk to the size of a <a href="/wiki/Billiard_ball" title="Billiard ball">billiard ball</a>, some areas of Earth such as large mountain ranges and oceanic trenches would feel like tiny imperfections, whereas much of the planet, including the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plains</a>, would feel smoother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[98]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Locally varies between 5 and 200 km.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Locally varies between 5 and 70 km.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-jaes41_3_379-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-jaes41_3_379_131-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Including the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Plate" title="Somali Plate">Somali Plate</a>, which is being formed out of the African Plate. See: <cite class="citation journal">Chorowicz, Jean (October 2005). <li id="cite_note-trench_depth-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-trench_depth_150-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is the measurement taken by the vessel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaik%C5%8D" title="Kaikō">Kaikō</a></i> in March 1995 and is considered the most accurate measurement to date. </mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0.01</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left({\frac {1}{3\cdot 332,946}}\right)^{\frac {1}{3}}=0.01}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\left({\frac {1}{3\cdot 332,946}}\right)^{\frac {1}{3}}=0.01" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ab5412b4744a2163206868fe8ec2726455c4785" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:24.249ex; height:7.343ex;"/></span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-solar_energy-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-solar_energy_212-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aphelion is 103.4% of the distance to perihelion. Due to the inverse square law, the radiation at perihelion is about 106.9% the energy at aphelion.</span></li>
</ol>

<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Earth</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">Local Interstellar Cloud</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">Local Bubble</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Gould_Belt" title="Gould Belt">Gould Belt</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Satellite_galaxies_of_the_Milky_Way" title="Satellite galaxies of the Milky Way">Milky Way subgroup</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster" title="Virgo Supercluster">Virgo Supercluster</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster" title="Laniakea Supercluster">Laniakea Supercluster</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Pisces%E2%80%93Cetus_Supercluster_Complex" title="Pisces–Cetus Supercluster Complex">Pisces–Cetus Supercluster Complex</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">Observable universe</a> <span style="font-size: 120%;">→</span> <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a><br/>
